<p><b>Sitakunda</b> (<a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a>: <span lang="bn">সীতাকুণ্ড</span> <i>Shitakunḍo,</i> <small>IPA:&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA" lang="bn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Bengali" title="Help:IPA/Bengali">[ʂitakunɖo]</a></span>) is an <a href="/wiki/Upazila" title="Upazila">upazila</a>, or administrative unit, in the <a href="/wiki/Chittagong_District" title="Chittagong District">Chittagong District</a> of Bangladesh. It includes one urban settlement, the <a href="/wiki/Sitakunda" title="Sitakunda">Sitakunda Town</a>, and 10 <a href="/wiki/Union_Councils_of_Bangladesh" class="mw-redirect" title="Union Councils of Bangladesh">unions</a>, the lowest of <a href="/wiki/Administrative_division" title="Administrative division">administrative units</a> in Bangladesh. It is one of the 15 upazilas, the second tier of administrative units, of the Chittagong District, which also includes 33 <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Bangladesh" class="mw-redirect" title="Administrative divisions of Bangladesh">thanas</a>, the urban equivalent of upazilas. The district is part of the <a href="/wiki/Chittagong_Division" title="Chittagong Division">Chittagong Division</a>, the highest order of administrative units in Bangladesh. Sitakunda is the home of the country's first <a href="/wiki/Nature_preserve" class="mw-redirect" title="Nature preserve">eco-park</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Alternative_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Alternative energy">alternative energy</a> projects, specifically <a href="/wiki/Wind_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Wind energy">wind energy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_power" title="Geothermal power">geothermal power</a>.
</p><p>Sitakunda is one of the oldest sites of human habitation in Bangladesh. During much of its history, it was ruled alternatively by various Buddhist rulers of <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a> in the east and Muslims rulers of <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> in the west. For a brief period in the 8th century, it was ruled by the Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Pala_Empire" title="Pala Empire">Pala Empire</a> of <a href="/wiki/History_of_India" title="History of India">India</a>. The eastern rulers originated from the <a href="/wiki/Rakhine_State" title="Rakhine State">Kingdom of Arakan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Mrauk_U" title="Mrauk U">Mrauk U</a> dynasty, <a href="/wiki/Magh_people" title="Magh people">Arakanese</a> pirates and the <a href="/wiki/Pagan_Kingdom" title="Pagan Kingdom">Pagan Kingdom</a>. The western rulers came from the <a href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_Bengal" class="mw-redirect" title="Sultanate of Bengal">Sultanate of Bengal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal</a> province (<a href="/wiki/Subah_(province)" class="mw-redirect" title="Subah (province)">Suba</a>) of Bangala. European rule of Sitakunda was heralded by Portuguese privateers in 16th and 17th centuries, who ruled together with the pirates; and the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a> in 18th and 19th centuries, who unified Sitakunda into the rest of the Chittagong District. <a href="/wiki/Diderul_Alam" class="mw-redirect" title="Diderul Alam">Diderul Alam</a> is the Current Member of parliament of Sitakunda
</p><p>Economic development in Sitakunda is largely driven by the <a href="/wiki/Dhaka-Chittagong_Highway" class="mw-redirect" title="Dhaka-Chittagong Highway">Dhaka-Chittagong Highway</a> and the railway. Though Sitakunda is predominantly an agricultural area, it also has the largest <a href="/wiki/Ship_breaking" title="Ship breaking">ship breaking</a> industry in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-pakecon_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pakecon-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-timeship_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeship-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The industry has been accused of neglecting <a href="/wiki/Workers%27_rights" class="mw-redirect" title="Workers&#39; rights">workers' rights</a>, especially concerning <a href="/wiki/Work_safety" class="mw-redirect" title="Work safety">work safety</a> practices and <a href="/wiki/Child_labor" class="mw-redirect" title="Child labor">child labor</a>. It has also been accused of harming the environment, particularly by causing <a href="/wiki/Soil_contamination" title="Soil contamination">soil contamination</a>. Sitakunda's ecosystems are further threatened by deforestation, over-fishing, and groundwater contamination. The upazila is also susceptible to natural hazards such as earthquakes, <a href="/wiki/Cyclone" title="Cyclone">cyclones</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">storm surges</a>. It lies on one of the most active seismic faults in Bangladesh, the Sitakunda–Teknaf fault.
</p><p>Sitakunda is renowned for its numerous <a href="/wiki/Islamic" class="mw-redirect" title="Islamic">Islamic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhist" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> shrines. It has 280 mosques, 8 <i><a href="/wiki/Mazar_(mausoleum)" title="Mazar (mausoleum)">mazars</a></i>, 49 Hindu temples, 4 <a href="/wiki/Ashram" title="Ashram">ashrams</a>, and 3 Buddhist temples. Among its notable religious sites are the <a href="/wiki/Chandranath_Temple" title="Chandranath Temple">Chandranath Temple</a> (a <i><a href="/wiki/Shakti_Peetha" class="mw-redirect" title="Shakti Peetha">Shakti Peetha</a></i> or holy pilgrimage site), Vidarshanaram Vihara (founded by the scholar <a href="/wiki/Prajnalok_Mahasthavir" title="Prajnalok Mahasthavir">Prajnalok Mahasthavir</a>), and the Hammadyar Mosque (founded by <a href="/wiki/Sultan" title="Sultan">Sultan</a> <a href="/wiki/Ghiyasuddin_Mahmud_Shah" title="Ghiyasuddin Mahmud Shah">Ghiyasuddin Mahmud Shah</a>). The attraction of Sitakunda as a tourist destination is elevated by these pilgrimage sites along with the hill range and the eco-park. Despite its diverse population, the area has gone through episodes of <a href="/wiki/Communalism_(South_Asia)" title="Communalism (South Asia)">communal strife</a>, including attacks on places of worship. There have been reports of activity by the Islamic militant group <a href="/wiki/Jama%27atul_Mujahideen_Bangladesh" class="mw-redirect" title="Jama&#39;atul Mujahideen Bangladesh">Jama'atul Mujahideen Bangladesh</a> since the early 2000s.<sup id="cite_ref-terrport_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-terrport-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-terrstar_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-terrstar-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sitakunda_Upazila&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Moored_boats_from_Arakan_or_Chittagong.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Moored_boats_from_Arakan_or_Chittagong.jpg/220px-Moored_boats_from_Arakan_or_Chittagong.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Moored_boats_from_Arakan_or_Chittagong.jpg/330px-Moored_boats_from_Arakan_or_Chittagong.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Moored_boats_from_Arakan_or_Chittagong.jpg/440px-Moored_boats_from_Arakan_or_Chittagong.jpg 2x" data-file-width="682" data-file-height="517" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Moored_boats_from_Arakan_or_Chittagong.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early 19th-century painting of pirate boats anchored near Chittagong coast</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fragment_of_map_of_Chittagong_(Bengal).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Fragment_of_map_of_Chittagong_%28Bengal%29.jpg/220px-Fragment_of_map_of_Chittagong_%28Bengal%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Fragment_of_map_of_Chittagong_%28Bengal%29.jpg/330px-Fragment_of_map_of_Chittagong_%28Bengal%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Fragment_of_map_of_Chittagong_%28Bengal%29.jpg/440px-Fragment_of_map_of_Chittagong_%28Bengal%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="481" data-file-height="564" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fragment_of_map_of_Chittagong_(Bengal).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sitakunda coast featured in a map from 1818</div></div></div>
<p>The legends of the area state the sage <a href="/wiki/Bhargava" title="Bhargava">Bhargava</a> created a pond (<i>kunda</i>) for <a href="/wiki/Sita" title="Sita">Sita</a> to bathe in when her husband Lord <a href="/wiki/Rama" title="Rama">Ramchandra</a> visited during his exile in the forests. Sitakunda derived its name from this incident.<sup id="cite_ref-StarSit_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StarSit-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sitakunda has been occupied by humans since the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> era; tools associated with the prehistoric <a href="/wiki/Hoabinhian" title="Hoabinhian">Assam group</a> have been found throughout the area.<sup id="cite_ref-utexas_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-utexas-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In 1886, <a href="/wiki/Celt_(tool)" title="Celt (tool)">shouldered celts</a> manufactured from <a href="/wiki/Petrified_wood" title="Petrified wood">petrified wood</a> were discovered, as reported by Indian archaeologist <a href="/wiki/Rakhaldas_Bandyopadhyay" class="mw-redirect" title="Rakhaldas Bandyopadhyay">Rakhaldas Bandyopadhyay</a> in his book <i>Banglar Itihas</i>, or <i>History of Bengal</i>, (volume I, 1914).<sup id="cite_ref-BangPre_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangPre-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In 1917, British <a href="/wiki/Mineralogist" class="mw-redirect" title="Mineralogist">mineralogist</a> Dr. J. Coggin Brown uncovered more prehistoric celts.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Large quantities of pebbles have also been found, but archaeologists have not determined whether they were used in the construction of prehistoric tools.<sup id="cite_ref-BangPre_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangPre-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the 6th and 7th centuries <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">CE</a>, the Chittagong region was ruled by the <a href="/wiki/Rakhine_State" title="Rakhine State">Kingdom of Arakan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BangChit_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangChit-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> In the next century, it was briefly ruled by <a href="/wiki/Dharmapala_of_Bengal" class="mw-redirect" title="Dharmapala of Bengal">Dharmapala</a> (reign: 770–810) of the <a href="/wiki/Pala_Empire" title="Pala Empire">Pala Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ages_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ages-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The area was conquered in 1340 by Sultan <a href="/wiki/Fakhruddin_Mubarak_Shah" title="Fakhruddin Mubarak Shah">Fakhruddin Mubarak Shah</a> (reign: 1338–1349) of <a href="/wiki/Sonargaon" title="Sonargaon">Sonargaon</a>, who founded the first dynasty of the Sultanate of Bengal.<sup id="cite_ref-BangChit_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangChit-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> When Sultan <a href="/wiki/Ghiyasuddin_Mahmud_Shah" title="Ghiyasuddin Mahmud Shah">Ghiyasuddin Mahmud Shah</a> (reign: 1533–1538) of the last dynasty of the Sultanate of Bengal was defeated in 1538 by <a href="/wiki/Sher_Shah_Suri" title="Sher Shah Suri">Sher Shah Suri</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sur_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Sur Dynasty">Sur Dynasty</a>, the Arakanese captured the region again. <a href="/wiki/List_of_Arakan_kings" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Arakan kings">Batsauphyu</a> (reign: 1459–1482) of the <a href="/wiki/Mrauk_U" title="Mrauk U">Mrauk U</a> dynasty took advantage of the weakness of Sultan Barbak Shah of Bengal to lead the invasion.<sup id="cite_ref-BangAra_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangAra-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> In this period, Keyakchu (or <i>Chandrajyoti</i>), a prince of Arakan, established a monastery in Sitakunda.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Between 1538 and 1666, Portuguese privateers (known as <i>Firinghis</i> or <i>Harmads</i>) made inroads into Chittagong and ruled the region in alliance with <a href="/wiki/Magh_people" title="Magh people">Arakanese</a> pirates. During those 128 years, the eastern coast of Bengal became a home to pirates of Portuguese and Arakanese origins.<sup id="cite_ref-BangAra_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangAra-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ChitCit_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChitCit-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> For a brief period in 1550, it was taken over by <a href="/wiki/Pagan_Kingdom" title="Pagan Kingdom">Pagan</a> invaders.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In 1666, Mughal commander Bujurg Umed Khan conquered the area.<sup id="cite_ref-BangChit_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangChit-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BangAra_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangAra-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Along with the rest of <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a>, Sitakunda came under the rule of the <a href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="British East India Company">British East India Company</a> after the company's defeat of the <a href="/wiki/Nawab_of_Bengal" class="mw-redirect" title="Nawab of Bengal">Nawab of Bengal</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Plassey" title="Battle of Plassey">Battle of Plassey</a> in 1757. Rapid growth in the <a href="/wiki/Bengali_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengali people">Bengali</a> population since then resulted in an exodus of non-Bengali people from Sitakunda and its vicinity to the <a href="/wiki/Chittagong_Hill_Tracts" title="Chittagong Hill Tracts">Chittagong Hill Tracts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> During the <i>Ardhodaya Yog</i> movement, a part of the <a href="/wiki/Swadeshi_movement" title="Swadeshi movement">Swadeshi Indian independence movement</a>, the governance of Sitakunda was briefly in the hands of Indian nationalists when, in February 1908, they took over the central government in <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Kolkata</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> In 1910, Indian Petroleum Prospecting Company drilled here for hydrocarbon exploration, the first such activity in <a href="/wiki/East_Bengal" title="East Bengal">East Bengal</a>. In 1914, the first onshore <a href="/wiki/Oil_well#Types_of_wells" title="Oil well">wildcat well</a> in Bangladesh was drilled at Sitakunda anticline to a depth of 762 metres (2,500&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> By 1914, however, all four of the wells drilled had proven to be failures.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Rebellion_of_1857" title="Indian Rebellion of 1857">Indian Rebellion of 1857</a>, the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British colonial government</a> (British Raj) replaced the <a href="/wiki/Company_rule_in_India" title="Company rule in India">governance of the East India Company</a>. When the British withdrew in 1947, after creating the independent states of India and Pakistan, Sitakunda became a part of <a href="/wiki/East_Pakistan" title="East Pakistan">East Pakistan</a>. The potential for a <a href="/wiki/Ship_breaking" title="Ship breaking">ship breaking</a> industry first appeared in 1964 when Chittagong Steel House started scrapping <i>MD Alpince</i>, a 20,000 metric tons (19,684 long tons) Greek ship that had been accidentally beached near Fouzdarhat by a <a href="/wiki/Tidal_bore" title="Tidal bore">tidal bore</a> four years earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-shipman_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shipman-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BDNPollute_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BDNPollute-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CBS_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBS-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>  On 15 February 1950, Hindu pilgrims form all over <a href="/wiki/East_Bengal" title="East Bengal">East Bengal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tripura" title="Tripura">Tripura</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assam" title="Assam">Assam</a> arriving for <a href="/wiki/Maha_Shivaratri" title="Maha Shivaratri">Maha Shivaratri</a> were attacked by the Ansars and armed Muslim mobs and <a href="/wiki/Sitakunda_massacre" title="Sitakunda massacre">massacred at the Sitakunda railway station</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-sinha71_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sinha71-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-kamra67_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kamra67-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_Liberation_War" title="Bangladesh Liberation War">Bangladesh Liberation War</a> of 1971, Sitakunda was part of <a href="/wiki/List_of_Sectors_in_Bangladesh_Liberation_War" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Sectors in Bangladesh Liberation War">Sector 1</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Ziaur_Rahman" title="Ziaur Rahman">Ziaur Rahman</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Major_Rafiqul_Islam&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Major Rafiqul Islam (page does not exist)">Major Rafiqul Islam</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Mukti_Bahini" title="Mukti Bahini">Mukti Bahini</a>, the forces fighting for the independence of Bangladesh. The ship breaking industry began in earnest in 1974 when Karnafully Metal Works started scrapping <i>Al Abbas</i>, a Pakistani ship damaged in 1971, and flourished in the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-shipman_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shipman-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IndiShip_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IndiShip-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> As of 2007, Sitakunda had overtaken the ship breaking industries of India and Pakistan to become the largest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-pakecon_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pakecon-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-timeship_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeship-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 2000s, Islamic militant organization <a href="/wiki/Jama%27atul_Mujahideen_Bangladesh" class="mw-redirect" title="Jama&#39;atul Mujahideen Bangladesh">Jama'atul Mujahideen Bangladesh</a> (JMB) leader Siddikul Islam (also known as <i><a href="/wiki/Bangla_Bhai" title="Bangla Bhai">Bangla Bhai</a></i>) ran militant training centers in the upazila at which participants learned to make bombs and handle firearms.<sup id="cite_ref-terrstar_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-terrstar-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> One of his followers, Mahfuzul Huq, was captured on 21 February 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-terrport_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-terrport-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_and_climate">Geography and climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sitakunda_Upazila&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Geography and climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ChandranathHillandMandir2.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/ChandranathHillandMandir2.JPG/220px-ChandranathHillandMandir2.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/ChandranathHillandMandir2.JPG/330px-ChandranathHillandMandir2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/ChandranathHillandMandir2.JPG/440px-ChandranathHillandMandir2.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ChandranathHillandMandir2.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Sitakunda range</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sitakunda18.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Sitakunda18.jpg/220px-Sitakunda18.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Sitakunda18.jpg/330px-Sitakunda18.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Sitakunda18.jpg/440px-Sitakunda18.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sitakunda18.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Road sign marking <i>Sahasradhara</i> spring and the eco-park</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Seetakoond_Hill_(33071618721).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Seetakoond_Hill_%2833071618721%29.jpg/220px-Seetakoond_Hill_%2833071618721%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Seetakoond_Hill_%2833071618721%29.jpg/330px-Seetakoond_Hill_%2833071618721%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Seetakoond_Hill_%2833071618721%29.jpg/440px-Seetakoond_Hill_%2833071618721%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1968" data-file-height="1238" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Seetakoond_Hill_(33071618721).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Sahasradhara</i> spring in Mid 19th century</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sitakunda33.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Sitakunda33.jpg/220px-Sitakunda33.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Sitakunda33.jpg/330px-Sitakunda33.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Sitakunda33.jpg/440px-Sitakunda33.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sitakunda33.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Sahasradhara</i> spring in Early 21st century</div></div></div>
<p>Sitakunda Upazila occupies an area of 483.97 square kilometres (186.86&#160;sq&#160;mi),<sup id="cite_ref-census0_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census0-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> which includes 61.61 square kilometres (23.79&#160;sq&#160;mi) of forest.<sup id="cite_ref-census3_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census3-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> It is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Mirsharai_Upazila" title="Mirsharai Upazila">Mirsharai</a> to the north, <a href="/wiki/Pahartali_Thana" title="Pahartali Thana">Pahartali</a> to the south, <a href="/wiki/Fatikchhari_Upazila" title="Fatikchhari Upazila">Fatickchhari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hathazari_Upazila" title="Hathazari Upazila">Hathazari</a> and <a href="/wiki/Panchlaish_Thana" title="Panchlaish Thana">Panchlaish</a> to the east, and the Sandwip Channel in the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a> to the west.<sup id="cite_ref-BangSit_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangSit-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The Sitakunda range is a 32-kilometre (20&#160;mi) long ridge in the center of the upazila, which reaches an altitude of 352 metres (1,155&#160;ft) above sea level at Chandranath or Sitakunda peak, the highest peak in Chittagong District.<sup id="cite_ref-ChitCit_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChitCit-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BangPhis_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangPhis-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Part of Sitakunda is covered by the low hill ranges, while the rest is in the Bengal <a href="/wiki/Flood_plain" class="mw-redirect" title="Flood plain">flood plain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BangPhis_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangPhis-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> To the north, Rajbari Tila at 274 metres (899&#160;ft) and Sajidhala at 244 metres (801&#160;ft) are the highest peaks in this range, which drops abruptly to a height of less than 92 metres (302&#160;ft) in the vicinity of Chittagong City to the south.<sup id="cite_ref-BangPhis_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangPhis-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> About 5 kilometres (3&#160;mi) north of <a href="/wiki/Sitakunda_Town" class="mw-redirect" title="Sitakunda Town">Sitakunda Town</a> is the Labanakhya <a href="/wiki/Hot_spring" title="Hot spring">saltwater hot spring</a>, which has been proposed as a source of <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">geothermal energy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> There are two waterfalls in the hills: <i>Sahasradhara</i> (thousand streams) and <i>Suptadhara</i> (hidden stream).<sup id="cite_ref-coastuddin_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-coastuddin-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Both have been identified as sites requiring special attention for protection and preservation by the <a href="/wiki/National_Heritage_Foundation_of_Bangladesh" title="National Heritage Foundation of Bangladesh">National Heritage Foundation of Bangladesh</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>An area prone to <a href="/wiki/Cyclones" class="mw-redirect" title="Cyclones">cyclones</a> and storm surges,<sup id="cite_ref-ICZM_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICZM-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Sitakunda was affected by cyclones in 1960, 1963, 1970, 1988, 1991, 1994 and 1997; the cyclones of 29 May 1963, 12 November 1970, 29 April 1991 made <a href="/wiki/Landfall_(meteorology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Landfall (meteorology)">landfall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
The intra-deltaic coastline is very close to the <a href="/wiki/Transform_boundary" class="mw-redirect" title="Transform boundary">tectonic interface</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Plate" title="Indian Plate">Indian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Burma_Plate" title="Burma Plate">Burmese plates</a>, as well as the active Andaman–Nicobar fault system, and is often capable of generating <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunamis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-faultstar_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faultstar-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Cyclone preparedness measures are inadequate for the 200,000&#160;residents of Sitakunda who were estimated to be living in high risk areas after the 1991 cyclone. For every 5,000 people, Sitakunda has only one cyclone shelter, each of which is capable of holding 50 to 60&#160;people. Syedpur Union has eleven, Muradpur eight, Baraiyadhala seven, and Kumira five. Sitakunda municipality, Barabkunda, Bhatiary and Bansbaria have four shelters each. Salimpur has three and Sonaichhari Union has two shelters.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Chittagong Coastal Forest Department developed the <a href="/wiki/Shoal" title="Shoal">river bars</a> (<i>char</i> in Bengali) on the bank of the Sonaichhari channel adjacent to the Sitakunda coast into a kilometer-wide coastal <a href="/wiki/Mangrove" title="Mangrove">mangrove</a> plantation during 1989–90, to reduce the impact of cyclones.<sup id="cite_ref-starmang_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-starmang-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Although the site was initially unstable, rapid sediment accretion stabilised the soil, providing the coast with some protection. The cyclone of 1990 smashed about 25% of a 2-kilometre (1&#160;mi) sea-wall built using two-ton steel-reinforced concrete blocks, some of which were carried up to 100 metres (328&#160;ft) inland. In contrast, a mangrove plantation just south of the sea-wall sustained damage to less than 1% of its trees, most of which recovered within six&#160;months.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> The planted mangrove forest that helped Sitakunda to escape as one of the least damaged areas during the devastating <a href="/wiki/1991_Bangladesh_cyclone" title="1991 Bangladesh cyclone">1991 Bangladesh cyclone</a> is under threat from illegal tree-cutting by ship-breakers in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-starmang_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-starmang-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Annual average temperature is between 32.5&#160;°C (91&#160;°F) and 13.5&#160;°C (56&#160;°F), with an annual rainfall of 2,687 millimetres (106&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-BangChit_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangChit-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Along with Chittagong and Hathazari, in June 2007 Sitakunda was badly affected by <a href="/wiki/Mudslide" class="mw-redirect" title="Mudslide">mudslides</a> caused by heavy rainfall combined with the recent practice of hill-cutting.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Akbar_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Akbar-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The mean annual wind speed recorded in Sitakunda between 1991 and 2001 was 1.8 knots (2&#160;mph),<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> as measured by the wind monitoring station built as part of a <a href="/wiki/Wind_energy" class="mw-redirect" title="Wind energy">wind energy</a> exploration project jointly run by the <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Engineering_Department" title="Local Government Engineering Department">Local Government Engineering Department</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_Center_for_Advanced_Studies" title="Bangladesh Center for Advanced Studies">Bangladesh Center for Advanced Studies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> A small 300-watt wind turbine, built by the government, provides electricity to fish farms.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sitakunda_Upazila&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Barnacles.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Barnacles.jpg/220px-Barnacles.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Barnacles.jpg/330px-Barnacles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Barnacles.jpg/440px-Barnacles.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Barnacles.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Balanus" title="Balanus">Balanus balanoides</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Structural_geology" title="Structural geology">geological structure</a> of Sitakunda, 70 kilometres (43&#160;mi) long and 10 kilometres (6&#160;mi) wide, is one of the westernmost structures of <a href="/wiki/Chittagong_District" title="Chittagong District">Chittagong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chittagong_Hill_Tracts" title="Chittagong Hill Tracts">Chittagong Hill Tracts</a>, delimited by the <a href="/wiki/Feni_River" title="Feni River">Feni River</a> in the north, the <a href="/wiki/Karnaphuli_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Karnaphuli River">Karnaphuli River</a> in the south, the <a href="/wiki/Halda_River" title="Halda River">Halda River</a> in the east and the Sandwip Channel in the west.<sup id="cite_ref-BangGeo1_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangGeo1-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The Sitakunda Range acts as a <a href="/wiki/Water_divide" class="mw-redirect" title="Water divide">water divide</a> between the Halda Valley and the Sandwip Channel. The 88 kilometres (55&#160;mi) -long Halda flows from <a href="/wiki/Khagrachari_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Khagrachari District">Khagrachari</a> to the Bay of Bangal, and is one of the six <a href="/wiki/Tributary" title="Tributary">tributaries</a> of Karnafuli, the major river in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> Sandwip Channel represents the northern end of the western part of the <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Bangladesh" title="Geology of Bangladesh">Chittagong-Tripura Folded Belt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The structure contains a thick sedimentary sequence of sandstone, shale and siltstone. The exposed sedimentary rock sequences except limestone, 6,500 metres (21,325&#160;ft) thick in an average, provide no difference in overall <a href="/wiki/Lithology" title="Lithology">lithology</a> of Chittagong and Chittagong Hill Tracts.<sup id="cite_ref-BangGeo1_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangGeo1-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The Sitakunda fold is an elongated, asymmetrical, box-type double plunging <a href="/wiki/Anticline" title="Anticline">anticline</a>. Both the gently dipping eastern and steeper western flanks of the anticline are truncated abruptly by the alluvial plain of the Feni River.<sup id="cite_ref-BangGeo1_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangGeo1-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> For a lack of infrastructure in Bangladesh, this anticline is one of the few regularly surveyed structures in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Syncline" title="Syncline">syncline</a> from Sitakunda separates the eastern end of the <a href="/wiki/Feni_District" title="Feni District">Feni Structure</a> located in the <a href="/wiki/Geological_fold" class="mw-redirect" title="Geological fold">folded flank</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Foredeep" title="Bengal Foredeep">Bengal Foredeep</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BangGeo1_51-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangGeo1-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Local experts consider the Sitakunda–Teknaf fault to be one of the two most active seismic faults in Bangladesh.<sup id="cite_ref-faultstar_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-faultstar-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> After the earthquake of 2 April 1762, which caused a permanent submergence of 155.4 square kilometres (60.0&#160;sq&#160;mi) of land near Chittagong and the death of 500 people in <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a>, two volcanoes are said to have opened in the Sitakunda hills.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Earth1_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Earth1-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> During a seismic tremor on 7 November 2007, fire broke out at the Bakharabad Gas Systems Limited in the Faujderhat area of the upazila when a pipeline was fractured.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> The Girujan Clay Formation runs through Sitakunda at a thickness of 168 metres (551&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-BangGeo2_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangGeo2-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In the Sitakunda hills, the Boka Bil Shale Formation contains <i><a href="/wiki/Ostreidae#Genera_and_species" title="Ostreidae">Ostrea digitalina</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ostreidae#Genera_and_species" title="Ostreidae">Ostrea gryphoides</a></i> and numerous plates of <a href="/wiki/Balanus" title="Balanus">Balanus</a> (a type of <a href="/wiki/Barnacle" title="Barnacle">barnacles</a>), fragments of <a href="/wiki/Arca_(bivalve)" title="Arca (bivalve)">Arca</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pecten_(bivalve)" title="Pecten (bivalve)">Pecten</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trochus" title="Trochus">Trochus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oliva_(gastropod)" title="Oliva (gastropod)">Oliva</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">corals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BangGeo2_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangGeo2-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Both formations were identified and named by early 20th-century British <a href="/wiki/Petroleum_geologist" title="Petroleum geologist">petroleum geologist</a> P. Evans.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demography">Demography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sitakunda_Upazila&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Demography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>According to the census of 2001, Sitakunda had a population of 298,528 distributed to 55,837 units of households (average household size 5.3), including 163,561 men and 134,967 women, or a gender ratio of 121:100.
The average population of component administrative units of the upazila are 4,072 for wards, 1,666 for <a href="/wiki/Mahalla" class="mw-redirect" title="Mahalla">mahallas</a>, 29,853 for <a href="/wiki/Union_Councils_of_Bangladesh" class="mw-redirect" title="Union Councils of Bangladesh">unions</a>, 5,060 for <a href="/wiki/Mouza" title="Mouza">mouzas</a> (revenue villages) and 5,060 for villages reported by the census.<sup id="cite_ref-census3_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census3-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Out of the 69 mauzas here, 8 have less than 50 households, while 27 have more than 600 households.<sup id="cite_ref-census3_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census3-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Of the villages, 8 have a population of less than 250, while 29 have more than 2,500.<sup id="cite_ref-census3_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census3-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> As of 2001<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Sitakunda_Upazila&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the population density of Sitakunda was 692 inhabitants per square kilometre (1,792/sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-census0_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census0-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Apart from the <a href="/wiki/Bengali_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengali people">Bengali</a> majority, there are a number of small communities of ethnic minorities in the area. Many of the resident <a href="/wiki/Rakhine_people" title="Rakhine people">Rakhine people</a> are believed to have settled here during the Arakanese rule of Chittagong (1459–1666), though the event is not historically traceable.<sup id="cite_ref-BangMagh_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangMagh-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The Rakhine population in <a href="/wiki/Khagrachari_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Khagrachari District">Khagrachari District</a> migrated from the surrounding area and built up their permanent abode at <a href="/wiki/Ramgarh_Upazila" title="Ramgarh Upazila">Ramgarh</a> in the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-BangMagh_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangMagh-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Other ethnic groups include the recently migrated <a href="/wiki/Tripuri_people" title="Tripuri people">Tripuri people</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In the District of Chittagong that includes Sitakunda, the population ratio by religion in 2001 was <a href="/wiki/Muslim" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> 83.92%, <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> 13.76%, <a href="/wiki/Buddhist" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> 2.01% and Christian 0.12%, with 0.19% following other religions. In 1981, it was Muslim 82.79%, Hindu 14.6%, Buddhist 2.23% and Christian 0.21%, with 0.19% following other religions.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Chittagonian_language" title="Chittagonian language">Chittagonian</a>, a derivative of <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a> spoken by 14&#160;million people mainly in the Chittagong district,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> is the dominant language.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Administration">Administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sitakunda_Upazila&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Sitakunda as a <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Bangladesh" class="mw-redirect" title="Administrative divisions of Bangladesh">thana</a> came into existence in 1879, and was renamed to Sitakunda Upazila in 1983.<sup id="cite_ref-urban_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-urban-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> It ranks third in area and sixth in population out of the 26 upazilas and thanas of Chittagong.<sup id="cite_ref-census3_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census3-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sitakunda_Town" class="mw-redirect" title="Sitakunda Town">Sitakunda Town</a>, with an area of 28.63 square kilometres (11.05&#160;sq&#160;mi) and a population of 36,650, is the administrative center and the sole municipality (<i>Pourashabha</i>) of Sitakunda Upazila.<sup id="cite_ref-census2_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census2-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Shafiul Alam is the mayor of the town, gaining a landslide win over his nearest contender M Abul Kalam Azad in the 2008 mayoral election.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The rest of the area is rural and organized into 10 union councils (<i><a href="/wiki/Union_parishad" class="mw-redirect" title="Union parishad">union parishads</a></i>), namely Banshbaria, Barabkunda, Bariadyala, Bhatiari, Kumira, Muradpur, Salimpur, Sonaichhari, Saidpur and Bhatiari Cantonment Area.<sup id="cite_ref-census3_32-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census3-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> The area is divided into 69 mauzas and 88 villages.<sup id="cite_ref-district-stats_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-district-stats-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>  Along with neighboring towns such as <a href="/wiki/Hathazari" class="mw-redirect" title="Hathazari">Hathazari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chittagong" title="Chittagong">Fateyabad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patiya" class="mw-redirect" title="Patiya">Patiya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boalkhali" class="mw-redirect" title="Boalkhali">Boalkhali</a>, Sitakunda Town was developed as a satellite town to relieve the increasing population pressure on Chittagong, with Bhatiari and Sadar unions selected as zones for industrialization, like <a href="/wiki/Chittagong" title="Chittagong">South Halishahar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chittagong" title="Chittagong">Kalurghat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-starmass_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-starmass-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> In the 2009 Upazila elections, Abdullah Al Baker Bhuiyan was elected the Upazila Chairman, while Advocate MN Mustafa Nur and Nazmun Nahar were elected vice chairmen.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sitakunda Upazila makes the 280th <a href="/wiki/List_of_Parliamentary_constituencies_in_Bangladesh" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Parliamentary constituencies in Bangladesh">electoral district</a> in Bangladesh, identified as Chittagong-3.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/2008_Bangladeshi_general_election" title="2008 Bangladeshi general election">2008 general election</a>, <span class="nowrap">A.B.M. Abul Kashem Master</span> of <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_Awami_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Bangladesh Awami League">Bangladesh Awami League</a> (AL) was elected as the <a href="/wiki/Jatiyo_Sangsad" class="mw-redirect" title="Jatiyo Sangsad">member of parliament</a>, defeating his nearest opponent <span class="nowrap">Mohammad Aslam Chowdhury</span> of <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_Nationalist_Party" title="Bangladesh Nationalist Party">Bangladesh Nationalist Party</a> (BNP).<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/2001_Bangladeshi_general_election" title="2001 Bangladeshi general election">previous election</a> held in 2001, Siddiki had defeated Kasem.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> M Akteruzzaman is the Upazila Nirbahi Officer, the chief executive of the upazila.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The upazila is served by a court presided over by a first-class <a href="/wiki/Magistrate" title="Magistrate">magistrate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The Power Development Board is responsible for supplying electricity to the upazila, but due to power outages the industries in the area are strictly constrained.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Anwarul Kabir Talukder, the State Minister for Power, lost his job on 29 September 2006 after hundreds of demonstrators in Sitakunda blocked the Dhaka–Chittagong highway in protest at the lack of electricity; violence also erupted elsewhere in Bangladesh.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In case of fire, the services are brought in from the neighboring city of Chittagong.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> A proposed Kumira–Sitakunda Hill Water Reservoir Project to supply safe drinking water is to be undertaken by the government.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sitakunda_Upazila&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shipbreakingbangladesh.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Shipbreakingbangladesh.jpg/220px-Shipbreakingbangladesh.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Shipbreakingbangladesh.jpg/330px-Shipbreakingbangladesh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Shipbreakingbangladesh.jpg/440px-Shipbreakingbangladesh.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1779" data-file-height="1189" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shipbreakingbangladesh.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ship breaking in Sitakunda</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fishing_boat_on_Bay_of_Bengal.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Fishing_boat_on_Bay_of_Bengal.JPG/220px-Fishing_boat_on_Bay_of_Bengal.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Fishing_boat_on_Bay_of_Bengal.JPG/330px-Fishing_boat_on_Bay_of_Bengal.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Fishing_boat_on_Bay_of_Bengal.JPG/440px-Fishing_boat_on_Bay_of_Bengal.JPG 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="525" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fishing_boat_on_Bay_of_Bengal.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fishing boat in the Bay of Bengal</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ship_breaking" title="Ship breaking">ship breaking</a> industry in Sitakunda has surpassed similar industries in India and Pakistan to become the largest in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-pakecon_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pakecon-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-timeship_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeship-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> As of August 2007, over 1,500,000 metric tons (1,476,310 long tons) of iron had been produced from the scrapping of about 20 ships in the 19 functional ship yards scattered over 8 square kilometres (3&#160;sq&#160;mi) along the coast of Sitakunda 8–10 kilometres (5–6&#160;mi) from <a href="/wiki/Chittagong" title="Chittagong">Chittagong</a>, near Fouzderhat. Local re-rolling mills, as well as similar mills, process the scrap iron.<sup id="cite_ref-IndiShip_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IndiShip-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WatMan_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WatMan-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ILO_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ILO-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Bangladesh, with no local metal ore mining industry of its own, is dependent on ship-breaking for its domestic steel requirements; the re-rolling mills alone substitute for import of about 1,200,000 metric tons (1,181,048 long tons) of billets and other raw materials.<sup id="cite_ref-IndiShip_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IndiShip-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> There are 70 companies registered as ship breakers in Chittagong, employing 2,000 regular and 25,000 semi-skilled and unskilled workers.<sup id="cite_ref-ILO_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ILO-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Organized under the Bangladesh Ship Breakers Association, (BSBA),<sup id="cite_ref-BDNPollute_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BDNPollute-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> these include companies within large local <a href="/wiki/Conglomerate_(company)" title="Conglomerate (company)">conglomerates</a> that sought <a href="/wiki/ISO_9000" title="ISO 9000">ISO certificates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The industry has come under threat, both from a decline in the number of ships scrapped annually – down from 70–80 to about 20<sup id="cite_ref-WatMan_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WatMan-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> – and because of environmental and <a href="/wiki/Occupational_safety_and_health" title="Occupational safety and health">work safety</a> concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-CBS_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CBS-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> There have been complaints that journalists and human rights activists are being barred from the ship breaking yards.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The ship breaking industry is purportedly damaging the local ecology as well, taking a toll on the fish population and soil quality.<sup id="cite_ref-greenship_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-greenship-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> A survey conducted by students of the <a href="/wiki/Institute_of_Marine_Science" class="mw-redirect" title="Institute of Marine Science">Institute of Marine Science</a> of <a href="/wiki/Chittagong_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Chittagong University">Chittagong University</a> in 2007 revealed that the soil of the locality is polluted by heavy metals including mercury (0.5 to 2.7&#160;ppm), lead (0.5 to 21.8&#160;ppm), chromium (220&#160;ppm), cadmium (0.3 to 2.9&#160;ppm), iron (2.6 to 5.6&#160;ppm), calcium (5.2 to 23.2&#160;ppm) and magnesium (6.5 to 10.57&#160;ppm).<sup id="cite_ref-IndiShip_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IndiShip-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Safety standards in the industry are low; between 1995 and 2005, 150 workers were killed and 576 were <a href="/wiki/Mutilation" title="Mutilation">maimed</a> or injured.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The main causes of death were fire or explosion, suffocation and inhaling <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">CO<sub>2</sub></a>. These old ships also contain hazardous substances like asbestos, lead paint, heavy metals and <a href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_biphenyl" title="Polychlorinated biphenyl">PCBs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The workers are paid US$1.75 a day and have little access to medical treatment.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Among the workers, 41% of are aged between 18 and 22 years,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> and many are reported to be as young as 10 years of age.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> There have also been allegations of large quantities of steel and non-ferrous items, such as <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>, aluminum, copper, and bronze-<a href="/wiki/Amalgam_(chemistry)" title="Amalgam (chemistry)">amalgam</a> recovered from ship breaking being smuggled out of Bangladesh.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> There also are reports of pirates targeting <a href="/wiki/Tugboat" title="Tugboat">tugboats</a> pulling ships in.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Employment of local people is low in the industrial facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-zaka_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zaka-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The main occupations of the local people by industry are <a href="/wiki/Tertiary_sector_of_economic_activity" class="mw-redirect" title="Tertiary sector of economic activity">service</a> (28.76%), commerce (21.53%), and agriculture (24.12%).<sup id="cite_ref-census3_32-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census3-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> Out of 12,140.83 hectares (30,000.64 acres) of cultivable land 25.46% yield a single crop, 57.95% yield double and 16.59% a treble crop annually. Bean, melon, rubber and <a href="/wiki/Betel" title="Betel">betel</a> leaf are the main agricultural exports.<sup id="cite_ref-BangSit_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangSit-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Fishing has traditionally been an industry restricted to low <a href="/wiki/Caste" title="Caste">caste</a> Hindus belonging to the fisher class, although since the last decades of the 20th century an increasing number of Muslims have joined the sector.<sup id="cite_ref-latif_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-latif-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Due to the introduction of engine-powered boats and <a href="/wiki/Gill_net" class="mw-redirect" title="Gill net">gill nets</a>, there was a rise in fish catches between the 1970s and 1990s, especially in the major fishing season (mid-July to mid-November).<sup id="cite_ref-latif_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-latif-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Over-fishing, however, has depleted the fish population and some fish species are facing extinction in the area, leading to seasonal <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food insecurity</a> (February to April).<sup id="cite_ref-latif_98-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-latif-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> According to a 2001 survey, 4,000 people in Sitakunda were engaged in wild <a href="/wiki/Spawn_(biology)" title="Spawn (biology)">shrimp fry</a> collection, harvesting an average of five-and-a-half million fries a year.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sitakunda has a cement factory, 12 <a href="/wiki/Jute" title="Jute">jute</a> mills, 6 textile mills, 10 re-rolling mills, and 79 functional and defunct shipyards.<sup id="cite_ref-BangSit_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangSit-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WatMan_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WatMan-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Two of the operational jute mills are run by the <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_Jute_Mills_Corporation" title="Bangladesh Jute Mills Corporation">Bangladesh Jute Mills Corporation</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> and one has been sold to a <a href="/wiki/Private_sector" title="Private sector">private sector</a> company.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> To protest against privatization, workers of Hafiz Jute Mill, Gul Ahmed Jute Mill, MM Jute Mill and RR Jute Mill blocked the Dhaka–Chittagong Highway for seven hours in September 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> As early as 1953, Sitakunda was described as the location for one of only five <a href="/wiki/Poultry_farming" title="Poultry farming">poultry farms</a> in <a href="/wiki/East_Pakistan" title="East Pakistan">East Pakistan</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Tejgaon_Thana" title="Tejgaon Thana">Tejgaon</a> (Dhaka), <a href="/wiki/Narayanganj" title="Narayanganj">Narayanganj</a> (Dhaka), Jamalpur (<a href="/wiki/Bogra" title="Bogra">Bogra</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Sylhet" title="Sylhet">Sylhet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Some mining for sand from agricultural lands is carried out along the eastern side of the Dhaka–Chittagong road.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> Operators of local brick kilns are engaged in illegal hill cutting, a practice that was responsible along with heavy rainfall for the <a href="/wiki/2007_Chittagong_mudslide" class="mw-redirect" title="2007 Chittagong mudslide">2007 Chittagong mudslide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Akbar_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Akbar-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mudslide_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mudslide-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> The rural poor are supported by <a href="/wiki/Grameen_Bank" title="Grameen Bank">Grameen Bank</a> and NGOs such as <a href="/wiki/CARE_(relief)" class="mw-redirect" title="CARE (relief)">CARE</a>, <a href="/wiki/BRAC_(NGO)" class="mw-redirect" title="BRAC (NGO)">BRAC</a> and <a href="/wiki/Association_for_Social_Advancement" title="Association for Social Advancement">ASA</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BangSit_33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangSit-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport_and_communication">Transport and communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sitakunda_Upazila&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Transport and communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dhaka_Chittagong_Highway.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Dhaka_Chittagong_Highway.jpg/220px-Dhaka_Chittagong_Highway.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Dhaka_Chittagong_Highway.jpg/330px-Dhaka_Chittagong_Highway.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Dhaka_Chittagong_Highway.jpg/440px-Dhaka_Chittagong_Highway.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dhaka_Chittagong_Highway.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dhaka–Chittagong Highway</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Dhaka%E2%80%93Chittagong_Highway" class="mw-redirect" title="Dhaka–Chittagong Highway">Dhaka–Chittagong Highway</a> runs through Sitakunda, connecting the two largest cities in Bangladesh. A workshop conducted by <a href="/wiki/Asian_Development_Bank" title="Asian Development Bank">Asian Development Bank</a> (ADB) estimated that improving the highway would increase Bangladesh's GDP by 1% and its foreign trade by 20%.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> This roadlink between the two cities existed in the pre-railway days<sup id="cite_ref-BangRail1_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangRail1-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> and has been identified as a part of the medieval southern <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> In 2006, ADB and the World Bank announced a plan to help Bangladesh build a second highway between Dhaka and Chittagong,<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> which would be a part of the <a href="/wiki/Asian_Highway_Network" title="Asian Highway Network">Asian Highway Network</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, the rail transportation system drove developments in Chittagong and the surrounding areas, including Sitakunda.<sup id="cite_ref-starmass_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-starmass-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Rail_track" class="mw-redirect" title="Rail track">rail tracks</a> were established as part of the <a href="/wiki/Bengal_Assam_Railway" class="mw-redirect" title="Bengal Assam Railway">Bengal Assam Railway</a> in 1898, originally running from Chittagong to <a href="/wiki/Badarpur_Railway_Town" title="Badarpur Railway Town">Badarpur</a>, with branches to <a href="/wiki/Silchar" title="Silchar">Silchar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laksam_Upazila" title="Laksam Upazila">Laksam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BangRail1_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangRail1-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> In September 1878, Sitakunda was included in the East Bengal Circle of Railway Mail Service (RMS) along with rest of the district.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> By 1904, the track system was extended to <a href="/wiki/Chandpur_Sadar_Upazila" title="Chandpur Sadar Upazila">Chandpur</a> to connect <a href="/wiki/River_boat" class="mw-redirect" title="River boat">river boat</a> traffic between <a href="/wiki/Goalandaghat_Upazila" class="mw-redirect" title="Goalandaghat Upazila">Goalanda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Kolkata</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BangRail1_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangRail1-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Approximately 37 kilometres (23&#160;mi) of railroads stop at six rail stations.<sup id="cite_ref-BangSit_33-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangSit-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Currently, there is no express train service between Sitakunda and Chittagong, though intercity expresses (Sylhet–Chittagong, Chandpur–Chittagong, and Dhaka–Chittagong) stop at Sitakunda station and carry a small share of the commuter traffic load.<sup id="cite_ref-starmass_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-starmass-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> By 2003, there were a total of 112 kilometres (70&#160;mi) of paved roads in the upazila, along with 256 kilometres (159&#160;mi) of mud roads, as well as five <a href="/wiki/Ferry_slip" title="Ferry slip">ferry-gauts</a> or river docks for the use of <a href="/wiki/Barge" title="Barge">barge</a>-type ferryboats. The traditional <a href="/wiki/Bullock_cart" title="Bullock cart">bullock carts</a> are now rarely seen in the upazila.<sup id="cite_ref-BangSit_33-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangSit-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sitakunda was to be the landing station for a <a href="/wiki/Submarine_communications_cable" title="Submarine communications cable">submarine communications cable</a>, but the cable now comes ashore at <a href="/wiki/Cox%27s_Bazar" title="Cox&#39;s Bazar">Cox's Bazar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> The cable has frequently been severed by miscreants, often in the Sitakunda area, since its installation on 21 May 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_NGOs_Network_for_Radio_and_Communication" title="Bangladesh NGOs Network for Radio and Communication">Bangladesh NGOs Network for Radio and Communication</a> (BNNRC) has brought internet services to the upazila by establishing Rural Knowledge Centres (RKC).<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/BTTB" class="mw-redirect" title="BTTB">BTTB</a> and <a href="/wiki/RanksTel" title="RanksTel">RanksTel</a> run telephone services in the upazila. The telephone area code for Sitakunda is 3028, which has to be added to Bangladesh area code +880 when making overseas calls, and the subscriber numbers consist of four digits locally.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pilgrimage_sites">Pilgrimage sites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sitakunda_Upazila&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Pilgrimage sites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Chandranath_Temple" title="Chandranath Temple">Chandranath Temple</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sitakunda06.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Sitakunda06.jpg/220px-Sitakunda06.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Sitakunda06.jpg/330px-Sitakunda06.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Sitakunda06.jpg/440px-Sitakunda06.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sitakunda06.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Entrance to Chandranath Temple</div></div></div>
<p>Sitakunda is a major site for pilgrimage in Bangladesh, as it features 280 mosques (including the Shah Mosque) 8 <a href="/wiki/Mazar_(mausoleum)" title="Mazar (mausoleum)">mazars</a> (including Baro Awlias Mazar, Kalu Shah Mazar, Fakir Hat Mazar, Shahjahani Shah Mazar), 49 Hindu temples (including Labanakhya Mandir, Chandranath Mandir, Shambunath Mandir), 3 <a href="/wiki/Ashram" title="Ashram">ashrams</a> (including
Sitakunda Shankar Math), and 3 Buddhist temples.<sup id="cite_ref-BangSit_33-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangSit-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The Hammadyar Mosque, located at the village of Masjidda on the banks of a <a href="/wiki/Water_tank" title="Water tank">tank</a><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> known as the Hammadyar Dighi, was built during the reign of Ghiyasuddin Mahmud Shah, the last Husain Shahi sultan of Bengal, as recorded by the inscription above the central entrance.<sup id="cite_ref-Bangmosque_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bangmosque-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The Sudarshan Vihara at village Mayani here, as well as the Vidarshanaram Vihara at village Mayani in <a href="/wiki/Patiya_Upazila" title="Patiya Upazila">Patiya</a> were both established in 1922 by <a href="/wiki/Prajnalok_Mahasthavir" title="Prajnalok Mahasthavir">Prajnalok Mahasthavir</a> (1879–1971), an eminent Bangladeshi Buddhist preacher.<sup id="cite_ref-Bangprajna_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bangprajna-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to legend, <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a>'s wife <a href="/wiki/Dakshayani" class="mw-redirect" title="Dakshayani">Sati</a> <a href="/wiki/Self-immolation" title="Self-immolation">immolated</a> herself in the <a href="/wiki/Yajna" title="Yajna">yajna</a>-fire of her father <a href="/wiki/Daksha" title="Daksha">Daksha</a>, as a protest against Shiva's dishonor. The God became furious and started to dance the <i><a href="/wiki/Tandava" title="Tandava">Tāndava</a></i> with Sati's body on his shoulders.<sup id="cite_ref-HindGod_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HindGod-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Knowing that the dance of destruction was about to annihilate the world, <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a> cut the body of Sati to pieces with <a href="/wiki/Chakram" title="Chakram">Sudarshana Chakram</a>, his celestial weapon, thereby appeasing Shiva.<sup id="cite_ref-HindGod_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HindGod-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Each of 51 pieces of the body fell to earth, and the place where each piece fell became a holy center of pilgrimage or <i><a href="/wiki/Shakti_Peetha" class="mw-redirect" title="Shakti Peetha">Shakti Peetha</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-HindGod_120-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HindGod-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The legend goes that Sati's right arm fell near a now-extinct hot spring at the Chandranth peak in Sitakunda. The site is marked by the temple of Sambhunath just below the Chandranath temple on top of the peak, and it is a major <i><a href="/wiki/Tirtha_(Hinduism)" title="Tirtha (Hinduism)">tirtha</a></i> for Hindus in Bangladesh.<sup id="cite_ref-Bangpeetha_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bangpeetha-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Rajmala" title="Rajmala">Rajmala</a>, the temple of Chandranath received considerable endowments from the <a href="/wiki/Twipra_Kingdom" title="Twipra Kingdom">Twipra Kingdom</a> in the time of king <a href="/wiki/List_of_Tripuri_Kings" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Tripuri Kings">Dhanya Manikya</a>, who once attempted to remove the <a href="/wiki/Lingam" title="Lingam">lingam</a> from the temple to his kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-StarSit_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StarSit-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prem1_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prem1-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Poets from across the ages – from <a href="/wiki/Jayadeva" title="Jayadeva">Jayadeva</a> (circa 1200&#160;AD) to Nabinchandra Sen (1847–1909) – were said to be devoted to the temple.<sup id="cite_ref-StarSit_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StarSit-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prem1_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prem1-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Chandranath is within the jurisdiction of Gobardhan Math, which was founded, according to legends, by Padmacharya, a disciple of <a href="/wiki/Adi_Shankara" title="Adi Shankara">Shankaracharya</a> and founder of <i>Vana</i> and <i>Aranya</i> sects of the <a href="/wiki/Dashanami_Sampradaya" class="mw-redirect" title="Dashanami Sampradaya">Dashanami Sampradaya</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-StarSit_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StarSit-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prem1_123-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prem1-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> An International Vedic Conference was held from 15 to 17 February 2007 at Sitakunda Shrine (Tirtha) Estate in Sitakunda Chandranath Dham, on the occasion of the great Shiva Chaturdarshi (a Hindu festival in worship of Lord Shiva).<sup id="cite_ref-StarSit_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-StarSit-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prem1_123-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prem1-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> These temples have been subject to repeated attack and violation by Muslims,<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_Hindu_Buddhist_Christian_Unity_Council" title="Bangladesh Hindu Buddhist Christian Unity Council">Bangladesh Hindu Bouddha Christian Oikya Parishad</a> has asked for the pilgrims to be protected.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora_and_fauna">Flora and fauna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sitakunda_Upazila&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Flora and fauna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kans_grass_(Saccharum_spontaneum)_in_Sitakunda1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Kans_grass_%28Saccharum_spontaneum%29_in_Sitakunda1.jpg/220px-Kans_grass_%28Saccharum_spontaneum%29_in_Sitakunda1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Kans_grass_%28Saccharum_spontaneum%29_in_Sitakunda1.jpg/330px-Kans_grass_%28Saccharum_spontaneum%29_in_Sitakunda1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Kans_grass_%28Saccharum_spontaneum%29_in_Sitakunda1.jpg/440px-Kans_grass_%28Saccharum_spontaneum%29_in_Sitakunda1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kans_grass_(Saccharum_spontaneum)_in_Sitakunda1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kans grass (<i><a href="/wiki/Saccharum_spontaneum" title="Saccharum spontaneum">Saccharum spontaneum</a></i>) in Sitakunda</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sitakunda20.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Sitakunda20.jpg/220px-Sitakunda20.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Sitakunda20.jpg/330px-Sitakunda20.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Sitakunda20.jpg/440px-Sitakunda20.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sitakunda20.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sitakunda eco-park</div></div></div>
<p>While returning to <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Kolkata</a> after completing a floral survey, <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Dalton_Hooker" title="Joseph Dalton Hooker">Joseph Dalton Hooker</a> (1817–1911) carried out the first survey of Sitakunda's local flora, as recorded in his <i>Himalayan Journals</i>, in January 1851 (published by the <a href="/wiki/Calcutta" class="mw-redirect" title="Calcutta">Calcutta</a> <a href="/wiki/Great_Trigonometric_Survey" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Trigonometric Survey">Trigonometrical Survey</a> Office and Minerva Library of Famous Books; Ward, Lock, Bowden &amp; Co., 1891).<sup id="cite_ref-BangFlor_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangFlor-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The forests of the region are known to be evergreen type with a preponderance of deciduous species with a levelled distribution.<sup id="cite_ref-BangFlor_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BangFlor-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The topmost level consists of Garjan (<i><a href="/wiki/Dipterocarpus" title="Dipterocarpus">Dipterocarpus alatus</a></i>), Telsur (<i><a href="/wiki/Hopea_odorata" title="Hopea odorata">Hopea odorata</a></i>), Chapalish (<i><a href="/wiki/Artocarpus" title="Artocarpus">Artocarpus chaplasha</a></i>), Chundul (<i><a href="/wiki/Tetrameles_nudiflora" class="mw-redirect" title="Tetrameles nudiflora">Tetrameles nudiflora</a></i>) and Koroi or the Moluccan albizia (<i><a href="/wiki/Falcataria_moluccana" class="mw-redirect" title="Falcataria moluccana">Falcataria moluccana</a></i>).
